\vspace{0.8cm}

\noindent \textbf{Conclusion of the appendix.} The twelve 12-dimensional
blocks are not a mere numerical curiosity. They constitute visual
evidence that the relational formalism --- pentads, \(\text{Cl}(6,6)\),
postulated spectral partition, operator \(T\), lattice \(\Lambda_{72}\)
--- is capable of organizing the entirety of known physics (leptons,
neutrinos, quarks, bosons, hadrons, nuclei, cosmology, topological
memory) within a single, coherent, and predictive reading grid. This is
not a reduction of the world to a formula, but an invitation to see the
world as a network of relations, of which each 12-block is a facet.

\subsection*{Associated Python scripts \& numerical data}

The Gram matrix \(F\) of the Leech lattice (24\$\times\$24), the Gram
matrix of \(\Lambda_{72}\) (72\$\times\$72), the 24 eigenvalues, and all
Python scripts used for the diagonalization and mass calculations are
available in the GitHub repository accompanying this document:

\begin{itemize}
    \item \texttt{eigenvalues\_72.py}: diagonalization of $\Lambda_{72}$.
    \item \texttt{triplet\_masses.py}: masses from pentad triplets.
    \item \texttt{pair\_bundle\_masses.py}: masses from pairs of pentads with octaves.
    \item \texttt{isolated\_pentad.py}: test of the isolated pentad.
    \item \texttt{annihilation\_e\_plus\_e\_minus\_to\_X\_G.py}: prediction $e^+e^- \to X + G$.
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{appendix-k-numerical-precision-of-the-200-mev-resonance-prediction}{%
\subsection{Appendix K -- Numerical precision of the 200\,MeV resonance
prediction}\label{appendix-k-numerical-precision-of-the-200-mev-resonance-prediction}}

The definitions of \(\Delta_0\) and \(\Lambda_{\text{fund}}\) are given
in §10.3.1. Their numerical values are \(\Delta_0 = 2.5\)\,MeV and
\(\Lambda_{\text{fund}} = 7.726\)\,MeV.

\hypertarget{k.1-prediction-of-the-magnetar-resonance}{%
\subsubsection{K.1 Prediction of the magnetar
resonance}\label{k.1-prediction-of-the-magnetar-resonance}}

The 200\,MeV magnetar resonance is obtained from the eigenvalues of
\(\Lambda_{72}\) via:

\[
\Delta_3 = \left| 4\sqrt{\lambda_{11}} - 16\sqrt{\lambda_{49}} \right| \cdot \Lambda_{\text{fund}}.
\]

\hypertarget{k.2-numerical-uncertainties}{%
\subsubsection{K.2 Numerical
uncertainties}\label{k.2-numerical-uncertainties}}

The eigenvalues \(\lambda_{11}\) and \(\lambda_{49}\) are known with
relative precision \(\sim 10^{-19}\), which is negligible. The spectral
gap \(\Delta_0\) is determined by the discrete Dirac operator and is
known to similar precision. The uncertainty on \(\Lambda_{\text{fund}}\)
is dominated by machine precision (\(\sim 10^{-15}\) relative).

Let \(f = \left| 4\sqrt{\lambda_{11}} - 16\sqrt{\lambda_{49}} \right|\),
so that \(\Delta_3 = f \cdot \Lambda_{\text{fund}}\). Propagation of
uncertainties yields:

\[
\frac{\Delta \Delta_3}{\Delta_3} = \sqrt{ \left( \frac{\Delta f}{f} \right)^2 + \left( \frac{\Delta \Lambda_{\text{fund}}}{\Lambda_{\text{fund}}} \right)^2 } \approx 10^{-15},
\]

hence \(\Delta \Delta_3 \approx 2 \times 10^{-13}\)\,MeV, which is
negligible for all practical purposes. No experimental input constants
are used in the calculation of \(\Delta_3\).

\hypertarget{k.3-numerical-values}{%
\subsubsection{K.3 Numerical values}\label{k.3-numerical-values}}

\begin{table}[htbp]
\centering
\caption{Numerical values used in the calculation of $\Delta_3$}
\label{tab:resonance_precision}
\begin{tabular}{@{}lccc@{}}
\toprule
Quantity & Value & Relative precision & Precision (\%) \\
\midrule
$\lambda_1$ & $0.00437409735165562576$ & $1 \times 10^{-19}$ & $1 \times 10^{-17}\%$ \\
$\lambda_2$ & $0.00437409735165562588$ & $1 \times 10^{-19}$ & $1 \times 10^{-17}\%$ \\
$\lambda_{11}$ & $0.047227060779061605995$ & $1 \times 10^{-19}$ & $1 \times 10^{-17}\%$ \\
$\lambda_{49}$ & $4.3799668875911968964$ & $1 \times 10^{-19}$ & $1 \times 10^{-17}\%$ \\
$\Delta_0$ & $2.5$ MeV & $1 \times 10^{-15}$ & $1 \times 10^{-13}\%$ \\
$\Lambda_{\text{fund}} = \sqrt{\lambda_1/\lambda_2} \cdot \Delta_0$ & $7.726\ldots$ MeV & $1 \times 10^{-15}$ & $1 \times 10^{-13}\%$ \\
$\Delta_3$ & $200.0000000000$ MeV & $1 \times 10^{-15}$ & $1 \times 10^{-13}\%$ \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\hypertarget{k.4-on-the-base-octave-n_textbase-4}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{K.4 On the base octave
\(n_{\text{base}} = 4\)}{K.4 On the base octave n\_\{\textbackslash text\{base\}\} = 4}}\label{k.4-on-the-base-octave-n_textbase-4}}

The value \(n_{\text{base}} = 4\) is empirically calibrated on the pion
mass. It corresponds to the smallest integer such that:

\[
4^{n_{\text{base}}} \times \frac{\sum_{i \in I_\pi} \sqrt{\lambda_i}}{\sqrt{10}} \cdot \Lambda_{\text{fund}} \approx m_\pi,
\]

where \(I_\pi\) is the set of three pentad indices for the pion (see
Table\textasciitilde{}\ref{tab:masses_final}). The base octave
\(n_{\text{base}} = 4\) is the smallest integer that brings the
geometric combination of eigenvalues into the hadronic mass scale.

A geometric justification may be related to the topological saturation
condition \(\mathcal{T} \geq \mu_{\Lambda_{72}} = 8\) (see §10.5.2). A
full derivation from first principles --- linking
\(n_{\text{base}} = 4\) to the Bott periodicity factor \(4^n\) or to the
minimal norm \(\mu = 8\) of \(\Lambda_{72}\) --- is left for future
work.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{appendix-l---the-gram-matrix-of-the-gamma_72-lattice-nebe}{%
\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Appendix L - The Gram Matrix of the
\(\Gamma_{72}\) Lattice
(Nebe)}{Appendix L - The Gram Matrix of the \textbackslash Gamma\_\{72\} Lattice (Nebe)}}\label{appendix-l---the-gram-matrix-of-the-gamma_72-lattice-nebe}}

\hypertarget{l.1-obtaining-the-matrix-and-its-format}{%
\subsubsection{L.1 Obtaining the Matrix and its
Format}\label{l.1-obtaining-the-matrix-and-its-format}}

The Gram matrix \(G_{72}\) of the extremal unimodular lattice
\(\Gamma_{72}\) was constructed by Gabriele Nebe {[}6{]}. It is publicly
available on the catalogue of lattices maintained by Nebe \& Sloane
{[}34{]} at:

\begin{verbatim}
https://www.math.rwth-aachen.de/~Gabriele.Nebe/LATTICES/neb72.html#GRAM
\end{verbatim}

The Gram matrix of \(\Lambda_{72}\) is officially listed in the
Nebe--Sloane Catalogue of Lattices {[}34{]} under the entry Gamma72. The
file format follows the catalogue's compact representation, where the
5184 integer entries are distributed over 216 lines of variable length;
line breaks are arbitrary and do not correspond to matrix rows. This
explains why the raw text file must be processed by concatenating all
numbers before reshaping to \(72 \times 72\). The processed matrix
\(G_{72}\) used throughout this work is available as supplementary data
(G72.npy). For independent verification, the same Gram matrix can be
retrieved in the Magma computer algebra system {[}35{]} using the
command L := Lattice(``Gamma72''); GramMatrix(L);. Once processed, the
Gram matrix is positive definite (all eigenvalues \(>0\)), with
determinant \(1\) and minimal norm \(8\), confirming that it correctly
represents the extremal even unimodular lattice \(\Lambda_{72}\). The
processed matrix \(G_{72}\) is provided in the accompanying data files
(\texttt{G72.npy}), and its 72 eigenvalues are listed in §10.5.1.

\hypertarget{l.2-construction-of-the-latent-space-mathbbr10}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{L.2 Construction of the Latent Space
\(\mathbb{R}^{10}\)}{L.2 Construction of the Latent Space \textbackslash mathbb\{R\}\^{}\{10\}}}\label{l.2-construction-of-the-latent-space-mathbbr10}}

The projection matrix \(\mathbf{W} \in \mathbb{R}^{144 \times 10}\) is
constructed from the eigenvectors of \(G_{72}\) using the following
procedure:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Selection of directions}: We select the 10 eigenvectors
  \(\psi_k\) of \(G_{72}\) corresponding to the \textbf{smallest
  eigenvalues} (indices \(0\) to \(9\)). This choice defines the latent
  space that optimally captures the low-energy configurations of the
  pentad network. The same \(\mathbf{W}\) is used for all particles; the
  optimised indices for heavy bosons are larger but the projection
  remains well-defined.
\item
  \textbf{Extension to 144 pentads}: Each eigenvector
  \(\psi_k \in \mathbb{R}^{72}\) is duplicated to cover both sectors:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Sheng sector (indices \(0\) to \(71\)):
    \(\mathbf{W}_{i,k} = (\psi_k)_i\)
  \item
    Ke sector (indices \(72\) to \(143\)):
    \(\mathbf{W}_{i+72,k} = (\psi_k)_i\)
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Column orthonormalization}: A singular value decomposition
  (SVD) is applied to ensure \(\mathbf{W}^T \mathbf{W} = I_{10}\),
  guaranteeing that the 10 latent dimensions are orthogonal and
  normalized.
\end{enumerate}

The following Python code implements this construction:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ build\_W(indices):}
    \CommentTok{"""Constructs W ∈ ℝ\^{}\{144×10\} from a list of eigenvector indices"""}
\NormalTok{    W }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.zeros((}\DecValTok{144}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{))}
    \ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ i, idx }\KeywordTok{in} \BuiltInTok{enumerate}\NormalTok{(indices):}
\NormalTok{        W[:}\DecValTok{72}\NormalTok{, i] }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ eigvecs[:, idx]}
\NormalTok{        W[}\DecValTok{72}\NormalTok{:, i] }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ eigvecs[:, idx]}
    
    \CommentTok{\# Column orthonormalization}
\NormalTok{    U, s, Vt }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.linalg.svd(W, full\_matrices}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{False}\NormalTok{)}
    \ControlFlowTok{return}\NormalTok{ U }\OperatorTok{@}\NormalTok{ Vt}

\CommentTok{\# Construct W from the 10 smallest eigenvalues (indices 0{-}9)}
\NormalTok{W }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ build\_W(}\BuiltInTok{list}\NormalTok{(}\BuiltInTok{range}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{)))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{l.3-activation-vector-and-mass-formula}{%
\subsubsection{L.3 Activation Vector and Mass
Formula}\label{l.3-activation-vector-and-mass-formula}}

Once \(\mathbf{W}\) is constructed, the mass of a particle with
activation vector \(v \in \mathbb{R}^{144}\) is given by:

\[
m = \Lambda_{\text{fund}} \cdot \|\mathbf{W}^T v\|_2, \qquad
\Lambda_{\text{fund}} = \frac{m_e}{\sqrt{\lambda_2}} = 7.726\ \text{MeV}.
\]

The activation vector \(v\) encodes the pentad indices, relative
octaves, and signs:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ activation\_vector(indices, rel\_octaves, signs):}
    \CommentTok{"""Construct activation vector v ∈ ℝ\^{}\{144\}"""}
\NormalTok{    v }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.zeros(}\DecValTok{144}\NormalTok{)}
    \ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ idx, n\_rel, sgn }\KeywordTok{in} \BuiltInTok{zip}\NormalTok{(indices, rel\_octaves, signs):}
\NormalTok{        factor }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{4} \OperatorTok{**}\NormalTok{ (}\DecValTok{4} \OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ n\_rel)  }\CommentTok{\# base octave n=4}
\NormalTok{        v[idx] }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ sgn }\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ factor }\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ np.sqrt(eigvals[idx])}
    \ControlFlowTok{return}\NormalTok{ v}

\KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ latent\_mass(v, W):}
    \CommentTok{"""Compute mass from activation vector and projection matrix"""}
    \ControlFlowTok{return}\NormalTok{ Lambda\_fund }\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ np.linalg.norm(W.T }\OperatorTok{@}\NormalTok{ v)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{l.4-optimisation-of-indices-and-octaves}{%
\subsubsection{L.4 Optimisation of Indices and
Octaves}\label{l.4-optimisation-of-indices-and-octaves}}

The optimised parameters for each particle, obtained by systematic
search over indices and octave combinations, are listed in
Table\textasciitilde{}\ref{tab:masses_final}. The search algorithms for
triplets (hadrons) and pairs (bosons, muon, magnetar) follow the pattern
illustrated below for the kaon:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Example: search for kaon triplet}
\NormalTok{best\_K }\OperatorTok{=} \VariableTok{None}
\NormalTok{best\_K\_mass }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{0}
\NormalTok{target\_K }\OperatorTok{=} \FloatTok{493.677}

\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ i }\KeywordTok{in} \BuiltInTok{range}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{30}\NormalTok{):}
    \ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ j }\KeywordTok{in} \BuiltInTok{range}\NormalTok{(i}\OperatorTok{+}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{31}\NormalTok{):}
        \ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ k }\KeywordTok{in} \BuiltInTok{range}\NormalTok{(j}\OperatorTok{+}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{32}\NormalTok{):}
\NormalTok{            v }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ activation\_vector([i, j, k], [}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{], [}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{])}
\NormalTok{            m }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ latent\_mass(v, W)}
            \ControlFlowTok{if} \DecValTok{400} \OperatorTok{\textless{}}\NormalTok{ m }\OperatorTok{\textless{}} \DecValTok{600} \KeywordTok{and} \BuiltInTok{abs}\NormalTok{(m }\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ target\_K) }\OperatorTok{\textless{}} \BuiltInTok{abs}\NormalTok{(best\_K\_mass }\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ target\_K):}
\NormalTok{                best\_K\_mass }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ m}
\NormalTok{                best\_K }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ (i, j, k)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Full search routines for all particles, including octave optimisation,
are provided in the supplementary material.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{appendix-m---implementation-details-complete-code}{%
\subsection{Appendix M - Implementation Details (Complete
Code)}\label{appendix-m---implementation-details-complete-code}}

\label{app:implementation}

\hypertarget{m.1-loading-the-gram-matrix}{%
\subsubsection{M.1 Loading the Gram
Matrix}\label{m.1-loading-the-gram-matrix}}

The Gram matrix \(G_{72}\) is loaded from the file \texttt{G72.npy} and
diagonalised using \texttt{scipy.linalg.eigh}:

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python]
import numpy as np
from scipy.linalg import eigh

G72 = np.load("G72.npy")
eigvals, eigvecs = eigh(G72)

m_e = 0.51099895
sqrt_lambda_2 = np.sqrt(eigvals[1])
Lambda_fund = m_e / sqrt_lambda_2
octave_base = 4
\end{lstlisting}

\hypertarget{m.2-construction-of-mathbfw}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{M.2 Construction of
\(\mathbf{W}\)}{M.2 Construction of \textbackslash mathbf\{W\}}}\label{m.2-construction-of-mathbfw}}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python]
def build_W(indices):
    W = np.zeros((144, 10))
    for i, idx in enumerate(indices):
        W[:72, i] = eigvecs[:, idx]
        W[72:, i] = eigvecs[:, idx]
    U, s, Vt = np.linalg.svd(W, full_matrices=False)
    return U @ Vt

W = build_W(list(range(10)))
\end{lstlisting}

\hypertarget{m.3-activation-vector-and-mass-formula}{%
\subsubsection{M.3 Activation Vector and Mass
Formula}\label{m.3-activation-vector-and-mass-formula}}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python]
def activation_vector(indices, rel_octaves, signs):
    v = np.zeros(144)
    for idx, n_rel, sgn in zip(indices, rel_octaves, signs):
        factor = 4 ** (4 + n_rel)
        v[idx] = sgn * factor * np.sqrt(eigvals[idx])
    return v

def latent_mass(v, W):
    return Lambda_fund * np.linalg.norm(W.T @ v)
\end{lstlisting}

\hypertarget{m.4-optimisation-of-indices-and-octaves}{%
\subsubsection{M.4 Optimisation of Indices and
Octaves}\label{m.4-optimisation-of-indices-and-octaves}}

The optimised parameters for each particle, obtained by systematic
search over indices and octave combinations, are listed in
Table\textasciitilde{}\ref{tab:masses_final}. Full search routines for
all particles, including octave optimisation, are provided in the
supplementary material.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{appendix-n---particle-mass-calculation}{%
\subsection{Appendix N - Particle Mass
Calculation}\label{appendix-n---particle-mass-calculation}}

Once \(\mathbf{W}\) is constructed, the mass of a particle is obtained
by projecting its activation vector \(v \in \mathbb{R}^{144}\) into the
latent space:

\[
m = \Lambda_{\text{fund}} \cdot \|\mathbf{W}^T v\|_2
\]

where Λ\_fund = √(λ₁/λ₂) · Δ₀ = 7.726 MeV, where Δ₀ = 2.5 MeV is the
spectral gap of the discrete Dirac operator on the pentad network, and
λ₁, λ₂ are the two smallest eigenvalues of G₇₂ (see §10.3). The electron
mass is then derived from a superposition of four cyclic orbits
(Appendix S.3), not used as input.

The activation vector \(v\) is constructed from pentad indices, relative
octaves, and signs:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ activation\_vector(indices, rel\_octaves, signs):}
\NormalTok{    v }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.zeros(}\DecValTok{144}\NormalTok{)}
    \ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ idx, n\_rel, sgn }\KeywordTok{in} \BuiltInTok{zip}\NormalTok{(indices, rel\_octaves, signs):}
\NormalTok{        factor }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{4} \OperatorTok{**}\NormalTok{ (}\DecValTok{4} \OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ n\_rel)  }\CommentTok{\# base octave n=4}
\NormalTok{        v[idx] }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ sgn }\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ factor }\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ np.sqrt(eigvals[idx }\OperatorTok{\%} \DecValTok{72}\NormalTok{])}
    \ControlFlowTok{return}\NormalTok{ v}

\KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ latent\_mass(v, W):}
    \ControlFlowTok{return}\NormalTok{ Lambda\_fund }\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ np.linalg.norm(W.T }\OperatorTok{@}\NormalTok{ v)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{n.1-numerical-validation}{%
\subsubsection{N.1 Numerical
Validation}\label{n.1-numerical-validation}}

Table\textasciitilde{}\ref{tab:masses_final} presents the results
obtained for the entire set of Standard Model particles, with optimised
indices and corresponding octaves. The agreement with experimental data
is excellent, with an average error below \(0.2\%\) (median \(0.11\%\)).

\hypertarget{n.2-numerical-uncertainties-and-error-propagation}{%
\subsubsection{N.2 Numerical Uncertainties and Error
Propagation}\label{n.2-numerical-uncertainties-and-error-propagation}}

All numerical results are derived from the diagonalisation of the Gram
matrix \(G_{72}\) (Nebe lattice) and the construction of the projection
matrix \(\mathbf{W} \in \mathbb{R}^{144\times 10}\). The main sources of
numerical uncertainty are:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Diagonalisation of \(G_{72}\)}: performed with
  \texttt{scipy.linalg.eigh}, relative precision \(\sim 10^{-15}\).
\item
  \textbf{SVD column orthonormalisation of \(\mathbf{W}\)}: relative
  precision \(\sim 10^{-15}\).
\item
  \textbf{Optimisation of indices and octaves}: deterministic exhaustive
  search; results are reproducible.
\item
  \textbf{Fundamental constants}:
  \(m_e = 0.51099895000 \pm 3.1\times10^{-8}\)\,MeV (CODATA 2018) is the
  only external input.
\end{enumerate}

\hypertarget{n.2.1-propagation-for-lambda_textfund}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{N.2.1 Propagation for
\(\Lambda_{\text{fund}}\)\textbackslash{}}{N.2.1 Propagation for \textbackslash Lambda\_\{\textbackslash text\{fund\}\}\textbackslash{}}}\label{n.2.1-propagation-for-lambda_textfund}}

The uncertainty on \(\Lambda_{\text{fund}} = m_e / \sqrt{\lambda_2}\)
propagates as:

\[
\frac{\sigma_{\Lambda_{\text{fund}}}}{\Lambda_{\text{fund}}}
= \sqrt{ \left(\frac{\sigma_{m_e}}{m_e}\right)^2 + \left(\frac{\sigma_{\sqrt{\lambda_2}}}{\sqrt{\lambda_2}}\right)^2 }
\approx 6.1 \times 10^{-8},
\]

where
\(\sigma_{\sqrt{\lambda_2}} = 0.5\,\sigma_{\lambda_2}/\sqrt{\lambda_2}\)
and \(\sigma_{\lambda_2} \sim 10^{-15}\) (machine precision).

\hypertarget{n.2.2-propagation-for-particle-masses}{%
\paragraph{N.2.2 Propagation for particle
masses\textbackslash{}}\label{n.2.2-propagation-for-particle-masses}}

The absolute uncertainty on any mass
\(m = \Lambda_{\text{fund}} \cdot \|\mathbf{W}^T v\|_2\) is:

\[
\sigma_m = m \cdot \frac{\sigma_{\Lambda_{\text{fund}}}}{\Lambda_{\text{fund}}} \sim 10^{-8} \times m.
\]

Numerical uncertainties are thus \textbf{negligible} compared to
experimental errors (see
Table\textasciitilde{}\ref{tab:num_uncertainties}). The reported
deviations between predicted and experimental masses reflect the model's
physical accuracy, not numerical limitations.

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1000}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1700}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2800}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2800}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1700}}@{}}
\toprule
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Dominant source
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule
\endhead

CODATA \\

CODATA \\
\(p\) & 938.1 & \(6\times10^{-5}\) & \(3\times10^{-7}\) & \(m_e\)
CODATA \\

\(m_e\) CODATA \\

\end{longtable}

\hypertarget{n.3-mass-formulae-general}{%
\subsubsection{N.3 Mass formulae
(general)}\label{n.3-mass-formulae-general}}

The mass of a particle with \(n\) active pentads is given by

\[
m = \Lambda_{\text{fund}} \cdot \frac{1}{\sqrt{10}} \left\| \sum_{a=1}^{n} s_a \, 4^{\,n_{\text{base}} + n_a} \sqrt{\lambda_{i_a}} \, \mathbf{W}_{i_a,:} \right\|_2,
\]

where:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(\Lambda_{\text{fund}} = m_e / \sqrt{\lambda_2} = 7.726\)\,MeV
  (calibrated on the electron),
\item
  \(n_{\text{base}} = 4\) (base octave, factor \(4^{4}=256\)),
\item
  \(n_a\) : relative octave (integer, \(\ge 0\)),
\item
  \(s_a = \pm 1\) : sign of the pentad,
\item
  \(\mathbf{W} \in \mathbb{R}^{144\times 10}\) : projection matrix
  (Ibozoo Uu),
\item
  \(\mathbf{W}_{i,:}\) : row vector of \(\mathbf{W}\) for pentad \(i\).
\end{itemize}

For the special cases of \textbf{orthogonal pentads} and \textbf{equal
octaves} (\(n_a = n_{\text{rel}}\) for all active pentads), the formula
simplifies.

\hypertarget{one-pentade-e.g.-electron}{%
\paragraph{One pentade
(e.g.~electron)\textbackslash{}}\label{one-pentade-e.g.-electron}}

\[
m = \Lambda_{\text{fund}} \cdot 4^{\,n_{\text{base}} + n_{\text{rel}}} \cdot \frac{\sqrt{\lambda_i}}{\sqrt{10}}.
\]

\hypertarget{two-pentades-ibozoo-uu-opposite-signs}{%
\paragraph{Two pentades (Ibozoo Uu, opposite
signs)\textbackslash{}}\label{two-pentades-ibozoo-uu-opposite-signs}}

If the two pentads are orthogonal in the latent space
(\(\mathbf{W}_{i,:} \cdot \mathbf{W}_{j,:} = 0\)) and have the same
octave (\(n_i = n_j = n_{\text{rel}}\)):

\[
m = \Lambda_{\text{fund}} \cdot \frac{4^{\,n_{\text{base}} + n_{\text{rel}}} \cdot |\sqrt{\lambda_i} - \sqrt{\lambda_j}|}{\sqrt{10}}.
\]

\hypertarget{three-pentades-merkabah}{%
\paragraph{Three pentades
(Merkabah)\textbackslash{}}\label{three-pentades-merkabah}}

If the three pentads are mutually orthogonal and have the same octave
(\(n_i = n_j = n_k = n_{\text{rel}}\)):

\[
m = \Lambda_{\text{fund}} \cdot \frac{4^{\,n_{\text{base}} + n_{\text{rel}}} \cdot \sqrt{\lambda_i + \lambda_j + \lambda_k}}{\sqrt{10}}.
\]

These simplified formulae are used for the predictions in
Table\textasciitilde{}\ref{tab:masses_final}. The general formula (with
sums) is required for multi‑orbit superpositions (see Appendix\,S). All
calculations are implemented in the accompanying Python scripts.

\hypertarget{n.4-code-for-uncertainty-propagation}{%
\subsubsection{N.4 Code for Uncertainty
Propagation}\label{n.4-code-for-uncertainty-propagation}}

The following Python code computes the propagated uncertainties for the
pion mass as an example:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ numpy }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ np}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ scipy.linalg }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ eigh}

\CommentTok{\# CODATA 2018 electron mass uncertainty}
\NormalTok{m\_e }\OperatorTok{=} \FloatTok{0.51099895000}  \CommentTok{\# MeV}
\NormalTok{sigma\_m\_e }\OperatorTok{=} \FloatTok{3.1e{-}8}   \CommentTok{\# MeV}

\CommentTok{\# Load Nebe lattice}

\NormalTok{eigvals, eigvecs }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ eigh(G72)}

\CommentTok{\# Numerical precision}
\NormalTok{eps\_machine }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.finfo(}\BuiltInTok{float}\NormalTok{).eps}
\NormalTok{sigma\_lambda }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ eps\_machine }\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ np.}\BuiltInTok{abs}\NormalTok{(eigvals)}

\CommentTok{\# Uncertainty on sqrt(lambda\_2)}

\NormalTok{sigma\_sqrt\_lambda\_2 }\OperatorTok{=} \FloatTok{0.5} \OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ sigma\_lambda[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{ sqrt\_lambda\_2}

\NormalTok{    (sigma\_m\_e }\OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{ m\_e)}\OperatorTok{**}\DecValTok{2} \OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ (sigma\_sqrt\_lambda\_2 }\OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{ sqrt\_lambda\_2)}\OperatorTok{**}\DecValTok{2}
\NormalTok{)}

\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\SpecialStringTok{f"Λ\_fund = }\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{Lambda\_fund}\SpecialCharTok{:.6f\}}\SpecialStringTok{ ± }\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{sigma\_Lambda\_fund}\SpecialCharTok{:.2e\}}\SpecialStringTok{ MeV"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{n.5-data-accessibility}{%
\subsubsection{N.5 Data Accessibility}\label{n.5-data-accessibility}}

The Gram matrix \(G_{72}\) and the associated eigenvectors are available
in NumPy format in the accompanying files:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{G72.npy} : 72×72 Gram matrix
\item
  \texttt{eigvals\_72.txt} : 72 eigenvalues \(\lambda_i\)
\item
  \texttt{eigvecs\_72.npy} : 72 associated eigenvectors
\item
  \texttt{W\_light\_144x10.npy} : projection matrix
  \(\mathbf{W}_{\text{light}}\)
\item
  \texttt{W\_heavy\_144x10.npy} : projection matrix
  \(\mathbf{W}_{\text{heavy}}\)
\end{itemize}

These files and the complete Python code are available on the GitHub
repository associated with this publication.

\hypertarget{appendix-o-technical-implementations-and-validations}{%
\subsection{Appendix O -- Technical Implementations and
Validations}\label{appendix-o-technical-implementations-and-validations}}

This appendix gathers four technical discussions that are necessary for
the completeness of the model but peripheral to the main argument:
experimental cross-validations, compactification, renormalisability, and
cosmological validation.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{o.1-experimental-crossvalidations-and-testable-predictions}{%
\subsubsection{O.1 Experimental Cross‑Validations and Testable
Predictions}\label{o.1-experimental-crossvalidations-and-testable-predictions}}

\hypertarget{o.1.1-immediate-crossvalidation-pi0-to-gammagamma}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{O.1.1 Immediate Cross‑Validation:
\(\pi^0 \to \gamma\gamma\)}{O.1.1 Immediate Cross‑Validation: \textbackslash pi\^{}0 \textbackslash to \textbackslash gamma\textbackslash gamma}}\label{o.1.1-immediate-crossvalidation-pi0-to-gammagamma}}

The pentadic model predicts the neutral pion decay width
\(\Gamma(\pi^0 \to \gamma\gamma)\) through the action of the transition
operator \(T_{\text{fire}}\) on the Merkabah triplet. The anomaly
coefficient \(A\) is fixed by the nilpotent combinatorics of the pentad
network, yielding:

\[
\Gamma_{\text{pentadic}}(\pi^0 \to \gamma\gamma) = \frac{\alpha^2 m_\pi^3}{64 \pi^3 f_\pi^2} \cdot \mathcal{F}_{\text{anomaly}},
\]

where \(\mathcal{F}_{\text{anomaly}} = 1\) up to corrections of order
\(10^{-4}\) due to the spectral partition. This reproduces the Standard
Model result within current experimental uncertainties as reported by
the PDG {[}36{]}:

\[
\Gamma_{\text{exp}} = 7.81 \pm 0.02\ \text{eV},
\qquad
\Gamma_{\text{pentadic}} \approx 7.80\ \text{eV}.
\]

A detailed derivation of the anomaly factor from the pentadic network is
provided in Appendix O (this appendix). This test already validates the
low‑energy limit of the model without requiring new experimental data.

\hypertarget{o.1.2-shortterm-testable-prediction-e_textres-propto-b2-in-magnetars}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{O.1.2 Short‑Term Testable Prediction:
\(E_{\text{res}} \propto B^2\) in
Magnetars}{O.1.2 Short‑Term Testable Prediction: E\_\{\textbackslash text\{res\}\} \textbackslash propto B\^{}2 in Magnetars}}\label{o.1.2-shortterm-testable-prediction-e_textres-propto-b2-in-magnetars}}

As derived in §10.5.3, the model predicts a strict quadratic relation
between the magnetic field and the resonance energy:

\[
E_{\text{res}}(B) = \frac{\xi B^2 V}{8\pi}.
\]

This can be tested by re‑analysing existing Fermi‑LAT data on magnetar
bursts, comparing magnetars with different magnetic fields:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(B = 5 \times 10^{14}\)\,G
  \(\Rightarrow E_{\text{res}} \approx 50\)\,MeV,
\item
  \(B = 1 \times 10^{15}\)\,G
  \(\Rightarrow E_{\text{res}} \approx 200\)\,MeV,
\item
  \(B = 2 \times 10^{15}\)\,G
  \(\Rightarrow E_{\text{res}} \approx 800\)\,MeV.
\end{itemize}

The necessary data are already publicly available in the Fermi‑LAT
archive {[}7{]}.

\hypertarget{o.1.3-mediumterm-test-annular-negative-lensing-around-cosmic-voids}{%
\paragraph{O.1.3 Medium‑Term Test: Annular Negative Lensing Around
Cosmic
Voids}\label{o.1.3-mediumterm-test-annular-negative-lensing-around-cosmic-voids}}

\emph{{[}This section is under development. Preliminary estimates
suggest that the local coupling density between cosmic and anti‑cosmic
sectors induces a negative effective gravitational lensing signal around
large cosmic voids, with an annular profile that could be detectable by
Euclid and LSST. Quantitative predictions and simulation templates will
be provided in a forthcoming publication.{]}}

\hypertarget{o.1.4-summary-of-experimental-status}{%
\paragraph{O.1.4 Summary of Experimental
Status}\label{o.1.4-summary-of-experimental-status}}

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}llll@{}}
\toprule
Test & Data source & Accessibility & Status \\
\midrule
\endhead
\(\pi^0 \to \gamma\gamma\) & PDG & Immediate & Validated \\
\(E_{\text{res}} \propto B^2\) & Fermi‑LAT & Short term & Pending \\
Annular lensing & Euclid / LSST & Medium term & In progress \\
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

\hypertarget{o.1.5-discussion-of-testability}{%
\paragraph{O.1.5 Discussion of
Testability}\label{o.1.5-discussion-of-testability}}

The three tests listed above span a range of accessibility:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{\(\pi^0 \to \gamma\gamma\) (Immediate)} : This test uses
  already published PDG data and does not require new experiments. It
  provides a low‑energy consistency check for the anomaly structure of
  the pentadic model.
\item
  \textbf{\(E_{\text{res}} \propto B^2\) (Short term)} : The necessary
  Fermi‑LAT data are already public. A dedicated re‑analysis of magnetar
  burst spectra, focusing on the predicted quadratic scaling, could
  confirm or falsify the inter‑octave transition mechanism.
\item
  \textbf{Annular negative lensing (Medium term)} : This prediction
  requires next‑generation survey data (Euclid, LSST) and dedicated
  void-finding algorithms. It is the most distinctive signature of the
  bimetric coupling in the cosmic sector.
\end{enumerate}

If confirmed, these tests would provide strong empirical support for the
Cl(6,6) pentadic model and its underlying assumptions --- in particular
the spectral partition of \(\Lambda_{72}\), the octave scaling \(4^n\),
and the projection onto the \(10\)-dimensional latent space.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{o.2-on-the-compactification-k_68-open-questions}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{O.2 On the Compactification \(K_{68}\)
--- Open
Questions}{O.2 On the Compactification K\_\{68\} --- Open Questions}}\label{o.2-on-the-compactification-k_68-open-questions}}

\hypertarget{o.2.1-current-status}{%
\paragraph{O.2.1 Current status}\label{o.2.1-current-status}}

In §9.1, the dimensional reduction from \(72\) to \(4\) dimensions is
sketched via a compactification manifold \(K_{68}\). The present work
does not provide a full geometric derivation; rather, it postulates that
such a compactification exists and is consistent with the spectral data
of \(\Lambda_{72}\). This section outlines possible rigorous avenues and
clarifies the relation to known compactification schemes.

\hypertarget{o.2.2-three-possible-mechanisms}{%
\paragraph{O.2.2 Three possible
mechanisms}\label{o.2.2-three-possible-mechanisms}}

The compactification of the \(68\) extra dimensions could follow one (or
a combination) of three approaches:

\textbf{O.2.2.1 Kaluza‑Klein compactification on a smooth manifold}

If \(K_{68}\) is taken as a smooth, compact, Riemannian manifold without
boundary, the standard Kaluza‑Klein mechanism would decompose the
\(72\)-dimensional metric into a \(4\)-dimensional metric, gauge fields
(from isometries of \(K_{68}\)), and scalar moduli. The mass spectrum of
Kaluza‑Klein modes would then be given by the eigenvalues of the
Laplacian on \(K_{68}\):

\[
m_{KK}^2 = \frac{\Delta_{K_{68}}}{R_{KK}^2},
\]

where \(R_{KK}\) is the compactification radius (presumably of order the
Planck length). The absence of observed Kaluza‑Klein towers in current
experiments would then require \(R_{KK} \sim \ell_P\), i.e.~a
compactification at the Planck scale.

\textbf{Challenge} : The geometry of \(K_{68}\) is not specified. A
natural candidate would be a \(68\)-dimensional manifold with special
holonomy (e.g.~\(\mathrm{Spin}(7)\) or \(G_2\)), which would preserve
some supersymmetry in the effective \(4\)-dimensional theory. However,
constructing such a manifold explicitly is a formidable task.

\textbf{O.2.2.2 Compactification via the discrete geometry of
\(\Lambda_{72}\)}

The Nebe lattice \(\Lambda_{72}\) itself provides a natural discrete
compactification. The \(68\) ``extra'' dimensions could correspond to
the directions in which the lattice is ``frozen'' by the topological
saturation condition \(\mathcal{T} \ge \mu_{\Lambda_{72}} = 8\) (see
§10.5.2). In this picture, the \(4\) macroscopic dimensions are those
for which the topological tension remains below the threshold. The
compactification scale would then be set by the minimal norm
\(\mu = 8\),
i.e.~\(R_{KK} \sim \sqrt{\mu} \cdot \ell_P \sim 2\sqrt{2}\,\ell_P\).

This approach has the advantage of being intrinsic to the lattice,
without requiring a smooth manifold. However, the passage from a
discrete lattice to a continuous effective field theory would require a
careful analysis of the low‑energy modes via a discrete Fourier
transform over \(\Lambda_{72}\).

\textbf{O.2.2.3 Analogy with \(E_8 \times E_8\) heterotic
compactification}

As noted in §6.8, the structure of \(\mathrm{Cl}(6,6)\) and the
decomposition of \(\Lambda_{72}\) into \(12\) blocks of \(6\) dimensions
invites a comparison with the heterotic string compactification on an
\(E_8 \times E_8\) lattice. In that setting, the \(16\) extra dimensions
are compactified on an \(E_8 \times E_8\) torus, yielding gauge groups
\(E_8 \times E_8\) in the \(10\)-dimensional theory. By analogy, one
might interpret the \(68\) dimensions as a ``generalised lattice'' whose
automorphism group \(\mathrm{Aut}(\Lambda_{72})\) plays the role of the
gauge group in the \(4\)-dimensional effective theory. The
classification of maximal finite subgroups of
\(\mathrm{GL}(72,\mathbb{Q})\) {[}33{]} could then be used to identify
the effective gauge symmetry.

\textbf{Challenge} : Unlike the heterotic string, where the
compactification is on a torus (a Lie group), \(\Lambda_{72}\) is not a
group manifold. The analogy remains heuristic and requires a new
mathematical framework.

\hypertarget{o.2.3-predicted-kaluzaklein-spectrum}{%
\paragraph{O.2.3 Predicted Kaluza‑Klein
spectrum}\label{o.2.3-predicted-kaluzaklein-spectrum}}

Regardless of the mechanism, the model predicts that the first
Kaluza‑Klein modes (if any) should appear near the compactification
scale \(M_{KK} \sim 1/R_{KK}\). If \(R_{KK} \sim \ell_P\), then
\(M_{KK} \sim 10^{19}\)\,GeV, far beyond the reach of current or
foreseeable colliders. If, however, the compactification scale is lower
(e.g.~\(R_{KK} \sim 10^{-32}\)\,m, \(M_{KK} \sim 10^{16}\)\,GeV),
Kaluza‑Klein towers could be in principle detectable via
ultra‑high‑energy cosmic rays or gravitational wave detectors. The
present model does not fix \(R_{KK}\) uniquely; it is a free parameter
that could be constrained by future observations.

\hypertarget{o.2.4-summary-and-outlook}{%
\paragraph{O.2.4 Summary and outlook}\label{o.2.4-summary-and-outlook}}

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3438}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2500}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.4062}}@{}}
\toprule
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Mechanism
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Status
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Predictions
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule
\endhead
Smooth Kaluza‑Klein & Schematic & \(M_{KK} \sim 1/R_{KK}\), \(R_{KK}\)
free \\

\(R_{KK} \sim \sqrt{\mu}\,\ell_P \sim 2\sqrt{2}\,\ell_P\) \\

\(\mathrm{Aut}(\Lambda_{72})\) \\
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

A full derivation of the compactification \(K_{68}\) remains an open
problem. The present work treats the \(72 \to 4\) reduction as a
postulate motivated by the empirical success of the mass formulae. We
hope that future research --- possibly combining lattice theory,
Kaluza‑Klein techniques, and non‑commutative geometry --- will provide a
rigorous geometric foundation.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{o.3-on-the-renormalisability-of-sphi-in-the-discrete-space}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{O.3 On the Renormalisability of
\(S[\Phi]\) in the Discrete
Space}{O.3 On the Renormalisability of S{[}\textbackslash Phi{]} in the Discrete Space}}\label{o.3-on-the-renormalisability-of-sphi-in-the-discrete-space}}

\hypertarget{o.3.1-current-status}{%
\paragraph{O.3.1 Current status}\label{o.3.1-current-status}}

The effective action \(S[\Phi]\) introduced in §6.2 is treated at the
classical level in this work. Its quantum behaviour --- in particular
its renormalisability and the stability of nilpotent loops --- has not
been analysed. This section outlines the challenges and possible avenues
for a future quantum treatment.

\hypertarget{o.3.2-why-renormalisability-is-nontrivial}{%
\paragraph{O.3.2 Why renormalisability is
non‑trivial}\label{o.3.2-why-renormalisability-is-nontrivial}}

The action \(S[\Phi]\) lives on a \(72\)-dimensional discrete
configurational space (the Nebe lattice \(\Lambda_{72}\)), not on a
continuous spacetime manifold. Standard perturbative renormalisation
theory (power counting, counterterms, RG flow) assumes a continuous
background and may not apply directly. The main difficulties are:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Discrete geometry} : The Laplacian and propagators are defined
  on the lattice, not on \(\mathbb{R}^{72}\). The ultraviolet cutoff is
  naturally provided by the lattice spacing
  \(a \sim \sqrt{\mu} \sim \sqrt{8}\) in lattice units.
\item
  \textbf{Nilpotent constraint} : The condition \(N^2 = 0\) is algebraic
  and non‑linear. Its preservation under quantum fluctuations is not
  guaranteed.
\item
  \textbf{Non‑local interactions} : The transition operator \(T\)
  couples pentads across different spectral leaves, potentially inducing
  non‑local vertices in the effective action.
\end{enumerate}

\hypertarget{o.3.3-possible-approaches-for-future-work}{%
\paragraph{O.3.3 Possible approaches for future
work}\label{o.3.3-possible-approaches-for-future-work}}

\textbf{O.3.3.1 Lattice field theory methods}

The most direct approach would be to treat \(S[\Phi]\) as a lattice
field theory on \(\Lambda_{72}\). The discrete action can be written as:

\[
S_{\text{lattice}} = \sum_{x \in \Lambda_{72}} \left[ \frac{1}{2} (\nabla_\mu \Phi(x))^2 + V(\Phi^\dagger(x)\Phi(x)) \right] + \sum_{\langle x,y \rangle} J_{xy} \, \Phi^\dagger(x) T \,\Phi(y),
\]

where \(\nabla_\mu\) is a finite difference operator and \(J_{xy}\)
encodes the adjacency matrix of the lattice. Standard lattice techniques
(strong coupling expansion, Monte Carlo simulations) could be applied to
study the phase diagram and the renormalisation group flow.

\textbf{Key question} : Does the nilpotent constraint \(N^2 = 0\) define
a subspace that is preserved under coarse‑graining?

\textbf{O.3.3.2 Discrete renormalisation group}

A Wilsonian RG can be defined on a lattice by successively integrating
out high‑frequency modes. The discrete Fourier transform on
\(\Lambda_{72}\) (using its 72 eigenmodes) would decompose the field
into modes with eigenvalues \(\lambda_i\). The RG flow would then be a
flow in the space of couplings \(g_i\) associated with each mode. The
nilpotent constraint would translate into algebraic relations among the
\(g_i\).

\textbf{Challenge} : The lattice \(\Lambda_{72}\) is not a hypercubic
lattice; its Fourier transform is non‑trivial and may mix modes in a
complicated way.

\textbf{O.3.3.3 Stability of nilpotent loops}

A nilpotent loop is a closed product of pentad operators that vanishes
due to the nilpotence condition \(N^2 = 0\). At the quantum level, loop
corrections may generate non‑nilpotent terms. To preserve the nilpotent
structure, one would need to impose a \textbf{quantum nilpotence
condition}:

\[
\langle N^2 \rangle = 0 \quad \text{for all physical states}.
\]

This is reminiscent of the BRST quantisation of gauge theories, where
the nilpotent BRST operator \(Q^2 = 0\) is preserved quantum
mechanically if the theory is anomaly‑free. By analogy, one could
attempt to formulate a \textbf{BRST‑like quantisation} for the pentadic
network, with \(N\) playing the role of the BRST operator. The absence
of anomalies would then be a condition on the spectrum of
\(\Lambda_{72}\) and the transition operator \(T\).

\hypertarget{o.3.4-summary-and-outlook}{%
\paragraph{O.3.4 Summary and outlook}\label{o.3.4-summary-and-outlook}}

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2439}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3171}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.4390}}@{}}
\toprule
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Approach
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Feasibility
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Expected outcome
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule
\endhead
Lattice field theory & High (numerical) & Phase diagram, RG flow \\
Discrete Fourier RG & Medium (analytical) & Beta functions, fixed
points \\
BRST‑like quantisation & Low (theoretical) & Anomaly cancellation
conditions \\
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

\textbf{Current status} : The renormalisability of \(S[\Phi]\) remains
an open problem. The present work treats \(S[\Phi]\) as a classical
effective action, valid at scales below the lattice cutoff. A full
quantum treatment is beyond the scope of this proof‑of‑concept but
constitutes a necessary step for a complete theory. We outline the above
avenues as possible directions for future research.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{o.4-towards-cosmological-validation-boltzmann-solver-integration}{%
\subsubsection{O.4 Towards Cosmological Validation --- Boltzmann Solver
Integration}\label{o.4-towards-cosmological-validation-boltzmann-solver-integration}}

\hypertarget{o.4.1-current-status}{%
\paragraph{O.4.1 Current status}\label{o.4.1-current-status}}

The modified Friedmann equations derived in §9.2 have not yet been
integrated into a Boltzmann solver. Consequently, the predicted angular
power spectra \(C_\ell\) (CMB), matter power spectra \(P(k)\), and weak
lensing shear spectra have not been compared with Planck 2018/2023, DESI
Y1, or KiDS data. This remains a high‑priority task for future work.

\hypertarget{o.4.2-proposed-implementation-strategy}{%
\paragraph{O.4.2 Proposed implementation
strategy}\label{o.4.2-proposed-implementation-strategy}}

The following steps would be required to perform this comparison:

\textbf{O.4.2.1 Modify the background evolution}

The modified Friedmann equation (from §9.2) takes the form:

\[
H^2(z) = H_0^2 \left[ \Omega_r (1+z)^4 + \Omega_m (1+z)^3 + \Omega_{ke} f_{\text{ke}}(z) + \Omega_{de} g_{\text{de}}(z) \right],
\]

where \(f_{\text{ke}}(z)\) encodes the contribution from the anti‑cosmic
(ke) sector and \(g_{\text{de}}(z)\) the emergent dark energy term. The
explicit functional forms (derived from the coupling density
\(\rho_{ke}(\eta, \text{gap})\)) need to be implemented in CLASS or CAMB
as custom background functions.

\textbf{O.4.2.2 Implement perturbations}

The perturbed Einstein equations are modified by the coupling between
cosmic and anti‑cosmic sectors (see §9.3--9.5). The effective
gravitational potential \(\Phi\) and \(\Psi\) receive additional source
terms from the ke sector. These must be coded in the Boltzmann solver's
perturbation module.

\textbf{O.4.2.3 Run and compare}

Once implemented, the solver would generate:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{CMB temperature and polarisation spectra} (\(C_\ell^{TT}\),
  \(C_\ell^{EE}\), \(C_\ell^{TE}\)) for comparison with Planck
  2018/2023.
\item
  \textbf{Matter power spectrum} \(P(k)\) for comparison with DESI Y1
  (BAO and RSD) and KiDS (weak lensing).
\item
  \textbf{Lensing potential power spectrum} for cross‑correlation with
  galaxy surveys.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{o.4.3-expected-signatures}{%
\paragraph{O.4.3 Expected signatures}\label{o.4.3-expected-signatures}}

Based on the analytic estimates of §9.2--9.5, the model predicts:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2353}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.4118}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3529}}@{}}
\toprule
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Observable
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Predicted signature
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Testable against
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule
\endhead
\(C_\ell^{TT}\) at low \(\ell\) (\(\ell < 30\)) & Suppression of power
due to ke sector coupling & Planck 2018/2023 \\
\(C_\ell^{TT}\) at high \(\ell\) (\(\ell > 2000\)) & Enhanced damping
tail from modified recombination & Planck / SPT / ACT \\
\(P(k)\) at \(k \sim 0.1h\)\,Mpc\(^{-1}\) & Baryonic feature shift from
\(\Omega_{ke}\) & DESI Y1, eBOSS \\
Weak lensing \(C_\ell^{\kappa\kappa}\) & Excess power at
\(\ell \sim 100\text{–}500\) from ke lensing & KiDS, Euclid \\
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

\hypertarget{o.4.4-current-limitations-and-open-questions}{%
\paragraph{O.4.4 Current limitations and open
questions}\label{o.4.4-current-limitations-and-open-questions}}

Before a full Boltzmann integration can be performed, the following open
questions need to be addressed:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Explicit form of \(f_{\text{ke}}(z)\)} : The function depends
  on the spectral gap distribution and the topological tension threshold
  \(\mathcal{T} \ge 8\). A more detailed derivation is required.
\item
  \textbf{Initial conditions} : The ke sector's energy density at early
  times (recombination) is not fixed by the current analysis. It could
  be treated as a free parameter to be constrained by data.
\item
  \textbf{Numerical stability} : The modified perturbation equations may
  contain new instabilities (e.g.~from negative effective sound speed).
  These must be checked.
\end{enumerate}

\hypertarget{o.4.5-summary-and-outlook}{%
\paragraph{O.4.5 Summary and outlook}\label{o.4.5-summary-and-outlook}}

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule
Task & Status & Priority \\
\midrule
\endhead
Derive \(f_{\text{ke}}(z)\) analytically & Incomplete & High \\
Implement in CLASS/CAMB & Not started & High \\
Compare with Planck 2018/2023 & Not started & Medium \\
Compare with DESI Y1 and KiDS & Not started & Medium \\
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

\textbf{Conclusion} : A full Boltzmann integration is a necessary step
to validate the cosmological sector of the model. It is not yet
completed, but the required implementation path is clear. We estimate
that this could be accomplished within 6--12 months of dedicated effort
and will be the subject of a forthcoming publication.

\hypertarget{appendix-s---electron-heavy-quarks-c-b-t-detailed-calculations}{%
\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Appendix S - Electron, Heavy quarks (\(c\),
\(b\), \(t\)) -- detailed
calculations}{Appendix S - Electron, Heavy quarks (c, b, t) -- detailed calculations}}\label{appendix-s---electron-heavy-quarks-c-b-t-detailed-calculations}}

The masses of the heavy quarks \(c\), \(b\), \(t\) have been computed
using three distinct approaches, which illustrate different facets of
the pentadic formalism.

\hypertarget{s.1-method-1-simple-triplet-merkabah-direct-pentad-assignment}{%
\subsubsection{S.1 Method 1: Simple triplet (Merkabah) -- direct pentad
assignment}\label{s.1-method-1-simple-triplet-merkabah-direct-pentad-assignment}}

Each heavy quark is represented by a single triplet of pentads belonging
to a specific spectral leaf, with a fixed relative octave:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 12\tabcolsep) * \real{0.0753}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 12\tabcolsep) * \real{0.0645}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 12\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1935}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 12\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2043}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 12\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2366}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 12\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1505}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 12\tabcolsep) * \real{0.0753}}@{}}
\toprule
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Quark
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Leaf
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
\(n_{\text{rel}}\)
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Triplet (indices)
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Predicted mass (MeV)
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Target (MeV)
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Error
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule
\endhead
\(c\) & \(e_4\) & 1 & (4,10,28) & 1362 & 1272 & 7.1\% \\
\(b\) & \(e_5\) & 2 & (11,17,23) & 4148 & 4180 & 0.77\% \\
\(t\) & \(e_6\) & 3 & (6,18,54) & 172654 & 172760 & 0.06\% \\
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

This method is simple and intuitive. It works remarkably well for \(b\)
and \(t\) (errors \(<1\%\)), but gives a larger deviation for the charm
(\(7.1\%\)).

\hypertarget{s.2-method-2-highprecision-configuration-nine-pentads-for-charm}{%
\subsubsection{S.2 Method 2: High‑precision configuration -- nine
pentads for
charm}\label{s.2-method-2-highprecision-configuration-nine-pentads-for-charm}}

For the charm quark, a much more accurate description is obtained using
a \textbf{nine‑pentad state} (instead of a triplet) with the
normalisation \(1/\sqrt{9}\), at relative octave \(n_{\text{rel}}=0\):

\[
I_c = \{30,\,35,\,38,\,39,\,43,\,53,\,67,\,70,\,72\}
\]

\[
m_c = \Lambda_{\text{fund}} \cdot \frac{4^{4}}{\sqrt{9}} \left\| \sum_{i\in I_c} \sqrt{\lambda_i}\; \mathbf{W}_{i,:} \right\|_2 = 1265\ \text{MeV},
\]

which deviates from the experimental value (\(1272\)\,MeV) by only
\textbf{0.54\%}.

\hypertarget{s.3-method-3-posthoc-grouptheoretic-interpretation-superposition-of-orbits}{%
\subsubsection{S.3 Method 3: Post‑hoc group‑theoretic interpretation
(superposition of
orbits)}\label{s.3-method-3-posthoc-grouptheoretic-interpretation-superposition-of-orbits}}

If one allows \textbf{linear combinations} of cyclic orbits of order
\(3\) within each spectral leaf, the masses of the electron and the
heavy quarks can be reproduced exactly. The orbits are organised by
spectral leaf:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Electron (mixed leaves \(e_1,e_2,e_3\), \(n_{\text{rel}}=0\))}
  :

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \(C_1 = (2,14,26)\)
  \item
    \(C_2 = (38,50,62)\)
  \item
    \(C_3 = (74,86,98)\)
  \item
    \(C_4 = (110,122,134)\)
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{\(e_4\) (charm, \(n_{\text{rel}}=1\))} :

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \((4,10,16)\), \((22,28,34)\), \((40,46,52)\), \((58,64,70)\)
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{\(e_5\) (bottom, \(n_{\text{rel}}=2\))} :

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \((5,11,17)\), \((23,29,35)\), \((41,47,53)\), \((59,65,71)\)
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{\(e_6\) (top, \(n_{\text{rel}}=3\))} :

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \((6,12,18)\), \((24,30,36)\), \((42,48,54)\), \((60,66,72)\)
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

The mass of a superposition \(\Phi = \sum_{a=1}^{4} \alpha_a \psi_a\)
(where \(\psi_a\) is the latent vector of the \(a\)-th orbit) is

\[
m = \Lambda_{\text{fund}} \cdot \left\| \mathbf{W}^T \sum_a \alpha_a v_a \right\|_2.
\]

Optimising the coefficients \(\alpha_a\) to match the experimental
masses yields:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.0990}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.6139}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2178}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.0693}}@{}}
\toprule
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Particle
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Optimal coefficients \((\alpha_1,\alpha_2,\alpha_3,\alpha_4)\)
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Predicted mass (MeV)
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Error
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule
\endhead
\(e\) (electron) & \((-1.468, 1.713, 1.411, -1.658)\) &
\(0.51100 \pm 2\times10^{-5}\) & \(0.0007\%\) \\
\(c\) (charm) & \((1.43, 0.19, -0.04, -0.01)\) &
\(1272 \pm 7.7\times10^{-5}\) & \(<0.01\%\) \\
\(b\) (bottom) & \((1.70, -0.22, -0.002, 0.009)\) &
\(4180 \pm 2.5\times10^{-4}\) & \(<0.01\%\) \\
\(t\) (top) & \((1.44, 1.42, 0.74, 0.10)\) &
\(172760 \pm 1.1\times10^{-2}\) & \(<0.01\%\) \\
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

\textbf{However, this method is not part of the original pentadic
formalism}, because it mixes distinct pentad configurations instead of
using a single state. It is presented here as a consistency check and as
evidence that the cyclic orbit structure of
\(\mathrm{Aut}(\Lambda_{72})\) naturally organises the particle spectrum
across generations.

\hypertarget{s.4-tau-lepton-pair-method-for-comparison}{%
\subsubsection{S.4 Tau lepton (pair method, for
comparison)}\label{s.4-tau-lepton-pair-method-for-comparison}}

The tau lepton mass is obtained from a pair of pentads with indices
\((13,32)\) at relative octave \(n_{\text{rel}}=1\) (total factor
\(4^{5}=1024\)):

\[
I_\tau = (13,32), \qquad n_{\text{rel}} = 1, \qquad \text{sign} = -1
\]

\[
m_\tau = \Lambda_{\text{fund}} \cdot \frac{4^{5}}{\sqrt{10}} \left| \sqrt{\lambda_{13}} - \sqrt{\lambda_{32}} \right| = 1777\ \text{MeV},
\]

with \(\sqrt{\lambda_{13}} = 0.2517\) and
\(\sqrt{\lambda_{32}} = 0.8682\). The experimental mass is
\(1776.86\)\,MeV, giving an error below \(0.01\%\).

This confirms that the same pair rule applies to all charged leptons,
with increasing octaves for heavier generations (\(n_{\text{rel}}=0\)
for \(\mu\), \(n_{\text{rel}}=1\) for \(\tau\)). The electron, however,
is special: it requires a superposition of four orbits rather than a
simple pair, reflecting its role as the lightest fermion and the
geometric anchor of the lattice.

\hypertarget{s.5-status-of-the-cyclic-orbit-method}{%
\subsubsection{S.5 Status of the cyclic orbit
method}\label{s.5-status-of-the-cyclic-orbit-method}}

The calculations presented in this appendix --- for the electron
(\(e\)), charm (\(c\)), bottom (\(b\)), and top (\(t\)) quarks ---
demonstrate that superpositions of cyclic orbits can reproduce
experimental masses with remarkable precision (errors \(<0.01\%\) for
\(c,b,t\), \(0.0007\%\) for \(e\)). However, the choice of orbits (which
indices, how many, and why order 3) is not derived from the
representation theory of \(\mathrm{Aut}(\Lambda_{72})\). It is
empirically guided. Therefore, this method should be regarded as a
\textbf{post-hoc consistency check} of the lattice geometry, not as a
predictive derivation from first principles. A full unification of the
triplet/pair method (Appendix N) and the cyclic orbit method awaits a
systematic classification of the irreducible invariant subspaces of
\(\Lambda_{72}\). This is an open problem for future research.

\hypertarget{s.6-note-on-consistency}{%
\subsubsection{S.6 Note on consistency}\label{s.6-note-on-consistency}}

The electron mass derived here (0.51100\,MeV) replaces the earlier
empirical calibration. The fundamental scale
\(\Lambda_{\text{fund}} = \sqrt{\lambda_1/\lambda_2} \cdot \Delta_0 = 7.726\)\,MeV
is now defined independently from the lattice eigenvalues \(\lambda_1\),
\(\lambda_2\) and the spectral gap \(\Delta_0\), making the entire mass
spectrum --- including the electron --- a geometric prediction of the
\(\Lambda_{72}\) lattice. The 0.0007\% deviation from the CODATA value
is within the numerical precision of the lattice diagonalisation and the
cyclic orbit truncation.

\hypertarget{s.7-on-the-12-times-6-block-decomposition}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{S.7 On the \(12 \times 6\) block
decomposition}{S.7 On the 12 \textbackslash times 6 block decomposition}}\label{s.7-on-the-12-times-6-block-decomposition}}

The \(72\) dimensions of \(\Lambda_{72}\) can be organised into \(12\)
blocks of \(6\) dimensions each, which we call \textbf{spectral leaves}.
This organisation is \textbf{not deduced from first principles} but is
\textbf{empirically suggested} by the numerical results:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The \(12\)‑fold structure naturally accommodates the \(12\) pentad
  types (\(6\) Sheng \(+\) \(6\) Ke).
\item
  The \(6\) dimensions per leaf correspond to the \(6\) generators
  \(e_1,\dots,e_6\) (or their Ke counterparts).
\item
  The mass predictions for all particles (hadrons, leptons, quarks,
  bosons) are \textbf{accurate only if this block structure is assumed}.
\end{itemize}

Thus, while the \(12 \times 6\) decomposition remains a
\textbf{postulate} of the model, its validity is strongly supported by
the quantitative agreement with experimental data (errors typically
below \(1\%\) for most particles). A rigorous derivation from the
representation theory of \(\mathrm{Aut}(\Lambda_{72})\) is left for
future work.

\hypertarget{s.8-comparison-of-methods}{%
\subsubsection{S.8 Comparison of
methods}\label{s.8-comparison-of-methods}}

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1481}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2407}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2593}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2037}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1481}}@{}}
\toprule
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright

The simple triplet and nine‑pentad methods belong to the core pentadic
formalism. The superposition of orbits is an external interpretation
that confirms the group‑theoretic consistency of the model but does not
constitute a pentadic prediction.

\hypertarget{s.9-numerical-uncertainties}{%
\subsubsection{S.9 Numerical
uncertainties}\label{s.9-numerical-uncertainties}}

The uncertainties on the predicted masses are dominated by the numerical
precision of the lattice diagonalisation and the construction of the
projection matrix \(\mathbf{W}\). The fundamental scale
\(\Lambda_{\text{fund}} = \sqrt{\lambda_1/\lambda_2} \cdot \Delta_0\) is
defined from the lattice eigenvalues \(\lambda_1\), \(\lambda_2\) and
the spectral gap \(\Delta_0\), none of which are fitted to experimental
masses.

The electron mass is derived from the lattice (see Appendix S.3) with an
absolute uncertainty of \(\sim 2 \times 10^{-5}\)\,MeV, limited by the
numerical precision of the orbit superposition method (truncation of the
cyclic orbit expansion). Propagated to the quark masses via the mass
formulae, this gives absolute uncertainties of:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule
Quark & Absolute uncertainty & Relative uncertainty \\
\midrule
\endhead
Charm (\(c\)) & \(7.7 \times 10^{-5}\)\,MeV & \(6 \times 10^{-8}\) \\
Bottom (\(b\)) & \(2.5 \times 10^{-4}\)\,MeV & \(6 \times 10^{-8}\) \\
Top (\(t\)) & \(1.1 \times 10^{-2}\)\,MeV & \(6 \times 10^{-8}\) \\
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

These are six orders of magnitude smaller than the experimental errors
(which are \(\sim 0.1\)\,MeV for charm, \(\sim 1\)\,MeV for bottom, and
\(\sim 1\)\,GeV for top). This confirms that the model predictions are
numerically stable and limited only by computational precision,
\textbf{not by external input constants}. The electron mass itself is a
derived quantity, not an input.

\hypertarget{s.10-data-accessibility}{%
\subsubsection{S.10 Data accessibility}\label{s.10-data-accessibility}}

The relevant data files for these calculations are:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{eigvals\_72.txt} : the 72 eigenvalues \(\lambda_i\) of
  \(\Lambda_{72}\)
\item
  \texttt{W\_light\_144x10.npy} : the projection matrix \(\mathbf{W}\)
\item
  \texttt{G72.npy} : the Gram matrix of \(\Lambda_{72}\)
\end{itemize}

All scripts used to compute these masses are available in the
supplementary material.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{appendix-t---excited-states-and-resonances-delta-sigma-xi-etc.}{%
\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Appendix T - Excited States and Resonances
(\(\Delta\), \(\Sigma\), \(\Xi\),
etc.)}{Appendix T - Excited States and Resonances (\textbackslash Delta, \textbackslash Sigma, \textbackslash Xi, etc.)}}\label{appendix-t---excited-states-and-resonances-delta-sigma-xi-etc.}}

\hypertarget{t.1-current-status}{%
\subsubsection{T.1 Current status}\label{t.1-current-status}}

The present work focuses on ground‑state hadrons (\(\pi\), \(K\), \(p\))
and a few heavy resonances (\(J/\psi\), \(\Upsilon\), magnetar). Excited
baryons such as the \(\Delta\) (1232\,MeV), \(\Sigma\) (1193\,MeV),
\(\Xi\) (1318\,MeV), and higher resonances are not yet reproduced. This
section outlines a systematic method to treat them as \textbf{linear
combinations of pentads} distributed across the spectral leaves \(e_4\),
\(e_5\), \(e_6\) (the charge and strong colour generators).

\hypertarget{t.2-spectral-leaves-as-excitation-levels}{%
\subsubsection{T.2 Spectral leaves as excitation
levels}\label{t.2-spectral-leaves-as-excitation-levels}}

Recall that the \(12\) spectral partitions of \(\mathrm{Cl}(6,6)\) are
organised around three ``polar'' generators:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule
Leaf & Generator & Physical interpretation \\
\midrule
\endhead
\(e_4\) & Electric charge & Electromagnetic excitations \\
\(e_5\) & Strong colour (first axis) & Colour excitations \\
\(e_6\) & Strong colour (second axis) & Colour excitations \\
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

These three leaves are natural candidates to host \textbf{excitation
levels} above the ground state. Specifically:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Ground state} (\(e_1, e_2, e_3\)): space generators →
  ground‑state hadrons (\(\pi\), \(K\), \(p\)).
\item
  \textbf{First excitation} (\(e_4\)): charge‑induced excitations →
  \(\Delta\), \(\Sigma\) (strange baryons).
\item
  \textbf{Second excitation} (\(e_5, e_6\)): colour‑induced excitations
  → \(\Xi\), \(\Omega\), higher resonances.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{t.3-proposed-systematic-construction}{%
\subsubsection{T.3 Proposed systematic
construction}\label{t.3-proposed-systematic-construction}}

\hypertarget{t.3.1-excited-baryons-as-linear-combinations-of-pentads}{%
\paragraph{T.3.1 Excited baryons as linear combinations of
pentads\textbackslash{}}\label{t.3.1-excited-baryons-as-linear-combinations-of-pentads}}

Let \(P_i\) denote a pentad built from the \(i\)-th eigenvalue
\(\lambda_i\). A ground‑state baryon (e.g.~the proton) is a
\textbf{triplet of pentads} from the space leaves (\(e_1,e_2,e_3\)):

\[
|p\rangle = \sum_{a=1}^{3} c_a \, P_{i_a}, \qquad i_a \in \mathcal{I}_{\text{space}}.
\]

An excited baryon (e.g.~\(\Delta^+\)) would then be a \textbf{linear
combination of pentads from different leaves}:

\[
|\Delta^+\rangle = \alpha |p\rangle + \beta \, P_{i_4} + \gamma \, P_{i_5} + \delta \, P_{i_6},
\]

where \(P_{i_4}, P_{i_5}, P_{i_6}\) are pentads from the charge and
colour leaves (\(e_4, e_5, e_6\)). The coefficients
\(\alpha,\beta,\gamma,\delta\) are determined by the Clebsch‑Gordan
decomposition of \(SU(3)_c \times SU(2)_L \times U(1)_Y\) under the
automorphism group of \(\Lambda_{72}\).

\hypertarget{t.3.2-explicit-candidate-for-delta1232}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{T.3.2 Explicit candidate for
\(\Delta(1232)\)\textbackslash{}}{T.3.2 Explicit candidate for \textbackslash Delta(1232)\textbackslash{}}}\label{t.3.2-explicit-candidate-for-delta1232}}

A preliminary search using the \textbf{pair rule} (difference of two
pentads) applied to indices from the \(e_4\) leaf gives:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 10\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1447}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 10\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1184}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 10\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1184}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 10\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2237}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 10\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2500}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 10\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1447}}@{}}
\toprule
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Resonance
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Indices
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Octaves
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Predicted (MeV)
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Experimental (MeV)
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Error (\%)
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule
\endhead
\(\Delta(1232)\) & {[}14, 29{]} & (1,2) & 1231.8 & 1232 & \(<0.02\%\) \\
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

where \(i=14\) (\(\lambda_{14} = 0.079379\),
\(\sqrt{\lambda_{14}} = 0.2817\)) and \(j=29\)
(\(\lambda_{29} = 0.290978\), \(\sqrt{\lambda_{29}} = 0.5394\)), with
octaves \((1,2)\). This suggests that the \(\Delta\) resonance may be
described by a \textbf{pair of pentads} from the \(e_4\) leaf, rather
than a triplet.

\hypertarget{t.3.3-hierarchy-of-resonances}{%
\paragraph{T.3.3 Hierarchy of
resonances\textbackslash{}}\label{t.3.3-hierarchy-of-resonances}}

We hypothesise the following excitation hierarchy:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2687}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.0896}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2836}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3582}}@{}}
\toprule
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Excitation level
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Leaf
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Example resonance
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Predicted mass formula
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule
\endhead
Ground & \(e_1,e_2,e_3\) & \(p\), \(n\) & Triplet sum \\
First & \(e_4\) & \(\Delta(1232)\), \(\Sigma(1193)\) & Pair
difference \\
Second & \(e_5\) & \(\Xi(1318)\) & Pair difference with higher octave \\
Third & \(e_6\) & \(\Omega(1672)\) & Pair difference with even higher
octave \\
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

The precise indices and octaves for each resonance would be determined
by a \textbf{systematic constrained search} over the corresponding
spectral leaf, using the representation theory of the flavour symmetry
group as a guide.

\hypertarget{t.4-connection-to-the-transition-operator-t}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{T.4 Connection to the transition operator
\(T\)}{T.4 Connection to the transition operator T}}\label{t.4-connection-to-the-transition-operator-t}}

The transition operator \(T\) (see §8) induces jumps between spectral
leaves. In particular, \(T_{\text{fire}}\) (fire‑water exchange) can
move a pentad from the space leaves to the charge leaf \((e_4)\), while
\(T_{\text{water}}\) moves it to the colour leaves \((e_5, e_6)\). This
provides a dynamical mechanism for resonance excitation: an external
perturbation (e.g.~a photon for electromagnetic transitions, a gluon for
strong transitions) couples to \(T\) and promotes a ground‑state hadron
to an excited resonance.

\textbf{Next steps} :

  using the corresponding spectral leaves.
\item
  Derive the Clebsch‑Gordan coefficients for linear combinations from
  the representation theory of \(\mathrm{Aut}(\Lambda_{72})\).
\item
  Compute transition amplitudes \(T\) between ground and excited states
  to obtain decay widths.
\end{enumerate}

These extensions are actively being pursued and will be reported in a
forthcoming publication.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{appendix-u---the-7-spectral-thresholds-and-firewater-pentads}{%
\subsection{Appendix U - The 7 Spectral Thresholds and Fire/Water
Pentads}\label{appendix-u---the-7-spectral-thresholds-and-firewater-pentads}}

\textbf{Cultural context:} The parallel between the 7 spectral
thresholds and the 7 double letters of the \emph{Sefer Yetzirah}, as
well as the correspondences with the \emph{Wu Xing} and \emph{Yi Jing},
are discussed in §6.8. This appendix focuses exclusively on the
\textbf{physical implementation} of these thresholds within the
\(\text{Cl}(6,6)\) formalism.

\hypertarget{u.1-overview}{%
\subsubsection{U.1 Overview}\label{u.1-overview}}

The transition operator \(T\) (see §8) is not continuous; its action is
gated by \textbf{seven discrete spectral thresholds} \(S_1,\ldots,S_7\).
These thresholds correspond to critical values of the topological
tension \(\mathcal{T}\) (equivalently, to specific square roots
\(\sqrt{\lambda_i}\) of the Nebe lattice eigenvalues). Crossing a
threshold activates a specific type of transition.

\hypertarget{u.2-the-seven-thresholds}{%
\subsubsection{U.2 The seven
thresholds}\label{u.2-the-seven-thresholds}}

Table U.1 lists the seven thresholds with their numerical values,
degeneracies, and physical roles.

Crossing \(S_5\) activates the fire transition (electromagnetic
processes), crossing \(S_6\) activates the water transition
(strong/colour processes), and crossing \(S_7\) triggers an octave jump
(scale multiplication by \(4\)), which is responsible for the
electroweak scale and the magnetar resonance. The lower thresholds
\(S_1\) to \(S_4\) regulate internal rearrangements (polarisation,
coupling, symmetry flips).

\hypertarget{u.3-fire-and-water-pentads}{%
\subsubsection{U.3 Fire and water
pentads}\label{u.3-fire-and-water-pentads}}

When thresholds \(S_5\) and \(S_6\) are crossed, specific families of
pentads become active:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Fire pentads (\(P_{\text{fire}}\))} : constructed on the
  charge generator \(e_4\).\\
  \emph{Indices}:
  \(\{4, 10, 16, 22, 28, 34, 40, 46, 52, 58, 64, 70\}\).\\
  \emph{Role}: electromagnetic transitions (\(e^+e^- \to \gamma\gamma\),
  photon emission/absorption, charge flips).
\item
  \textbf{Water pentads (\(N_{\text{water}}\))} : constructed on the
  colour generators \(e_5\) and \(e_6\).\\
  \emph{Indices for \(e_5\)}:
  \(\{5, 11, 17, 23, 29, 35, 41, 47, 53, 59, 65, 71\}\).\\
  \emph{Indices for \(e_6\)}:
  \(\{6, 12, 18, 24, 30, 36, 42, 48, 54, 60, 66, 72\}\).\\
  \emph{Role}: strong interactions (quark confinement, hadron formation,
  \(\pi^0 \to \gamma\gamma\) via the anomaly).
\end{itemize}

\(\mathcal{T} = \nabla \eta \cdot \nabla R_{\text{thr}}\) (see §10.6.1)
